        <title>The draft genome of the C 3 panicoid grass species Dichanthelium oligosanthes | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Comparisons between C3 and C4 grasses often utilize C3 species from the subfamilies Ehrhartoideae or Pooideae and C4 species from the subfamily Panicoideae, two clades that diverged over 50 million years ago. The divergence of the C3 panicoid grass Dichanthelium oligosanthes from the independent C4 lineages represented by Setaria viridis and Sorghum bicolor occurred approximately 15 million years ago, which is significantly more recent than members of the Bambusoideae, Ehrhartoideae, and Pooideae subfamilies. D. oligosanthes is ideally placed within the panicoid clade for comparative studies of C3 and C4 grasses. We report the assembly of the nuclear and chloroplast genomes of D. oligosanthes, from high-throughput short read sequencing data and a comparative transcriptomics analysis of the developing leaf of D. oligosanthes, S. viridis, and S. bicolor. Physiological and anatomical characterizations verified that D. oligosanthes utilizes the C3 pathway for carbon fixation and lacks Kranz anatomy. Expression profiles of transcription factors along developing leaves of D. oligosanthes and S. viridis were compared with previously published data from S. bicolor, Zea mays, and Oryza sativa to identify a small suite of transcription factors that likely acquired functions specifically related to C4 photosynthesis. The phylogenetic location of D. oligosanthes makes it an ideal C3 plant for comparative analysis of C4 evolution in the panicoid grasses. This genome will not only provide a better C3 species for comparisons with C4 panicoid grasses, but also highlights the power of using high-throughput sequencing to address questions in evolutionary biology."/>

        <!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The draft genome of the C 3 panicoid grass species Dichanthelium oligosanthes"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Comparisons between C3 and C4 grasses often utilize C3 species from the subfamilies Ehrhartoideae or Pooideae and C4 species from the subfamily Panicoideae, two clades that diverged over 50 million years ago. The divergence of the C3 panicoid grass Dichanthelium oligosanthes from the independent C4 lineages represented by Setaria viridis and Sorghum bicolor occurred approximately 15 million years ago, which is significantly more recent than members of the Bambusoideae, Ehrhartoideae, and Pooideae subfamilies. D. oligosanthes is ideally placed within the panicoid clade for comparative studies of C3 and C4 grasses. We report the assembly of the nuclear and chloroplast genomes of D. oligosanthes, from high-throughput short read sequencing data and a comparative transcriptomics analysis of the developing leaf of D. oligosanthes, S. viridis, and S. bicolor. Physiological and anatomical characterizations verified that D. oligosanthes utilizes the C3 pathway for carbon fixation and lacks Kranz anatomy. Expression profiles of transcription factors along developing leaves of D. oligosanthes and S. viridis were compared with previously published data from S. bicolor, Zea mays, and Oryza sativa to identify a small suite of transcription factors that likely acquired functions specifically related to C4 photosynthesis. The phylogenetic location of D. oligosanthes makes it an ideal C3 plant for comparative analysis of C4 evolution in the panicoid grasses. This genome will not only provide a better C3 species for comparisons with C4 panicoid grasses, but also highlights the power of using high-throughput sequencing to address questions in evolutionary biology."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">The draft genome of the C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass species <i>Dichanthelium oligosanthes</i>
                  </h1>

                        <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Comparisons between C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> grasses often utilize C<sub>3</sub> species from the subfamilies Ehrhartoideae or Pooideae and C<sub>4</sub> species from the subfamily Panicoideae, two clades that diverged over 50 million years ago. The divergence of the C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass <i>Dichanthelium oligosanthes</i> from the independent C<sub>4</sub> lineages represented by <i>Setaria viridis</i> and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> occurred approximately 15 million years ago, which is significantly more recent than members of the Bambusoideae, Ehrhartoideae, and Pooideae subfamilies. <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is ideally placed within the panicoid clade for comparative studies of C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> grasses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We report the assembly of the nuclear and chloroplast genomes of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, from high-throughput short read sequencing data and a comparative transcriptomics analysis of the developing leaf of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, <i>S. viridis</i>, and <i>S. bicolor</i>. Physiological and anatomical characterizations verified that <i>D. oligosanthes</i> utilizes the C<sub>3</sub> pathway for carbon fixation and lacks Kranz anatomy. Expression profiles of transcription factors along developing leaves of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> were compared with previously published data from <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa</i> to identify a small suite of transcription factors that likely acquired functions specifically related to C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The phylogenetic location of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> makes it an ideal C<sub>3</sub> plant for comparative analysis of C<sub>4</sub> evolution in the panicoid grasses. This genome will not only provide a better C<sub>3</sub> species for comparisons with C<sub>4</sub> panicoid grasses, but also highlights the power of using high-throughput sequencing to address questions in evolutionary biology.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The availability of complete genome sequences from multiple lineages is enabling a much deeper understanding of both the mechanistic basis of evolution and the diversification of gene regulatory networks. Furthermore, the breadth of genome sequences available provides opportunities to utilize non-model species in comparative genomics [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ellegren H. Genome sequencing and population genomics in non-model organisms. Trends Ecol Evol. 2014;29:5163." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33287e799">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Freeling M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Trop Plant Biol. 2008;1:18190." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33287e802">2</a>]. Comparative approaches are made more powerful by sampling across the phylogenetic tree, particularly in cases of convergent evolution, and provide insight into the networks that underpin complex traits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Koonin EV, Aravind L, Kondrashov AS. The impact of comparative genomics on our understanding of evolution. Cell. 2000;101:5736." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d33287e805">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Christin PA, Salamin N, Savolainen V, Duvall MR, Besnard G. C4 Photosynthesis evolved in grasses via parallel adaptive genetic changes. Curr Biol. 2007;17:12417." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33287e808">4</a>]. High-throughput sequencing facilitates deep transcriptomic and genomic surveys, which can be leveraged to deduce the evolution of gene families by duplication and subsequent neofunctionalization and subfunctionalization of individual gene copies.</p><p>Growing concern over food and energy security has spurred translational research to increase the productivity and sustainability of crops. Optimization of photosynthesis is one approach that has the potential to greatly increase crop yields [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zhu XG, Long SP, Ort DR. Improving photosynthetic efficiency for greater yield. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2010;61:23561." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33287e814">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ort DR, Merchant SS, Alric J, Barkan A, Blankenship RE, Bock R, et al. Redesigning photosynthesis to sustainably meet global food and bioenergy demand. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112:852936." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33287e817">6</a>]. Specifically, several groups are investigating the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> from C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis with the objective of installing C<sub>4</sub> traits into C<sub>3</sub> species to improve yield [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sage RF, Zhu XG. Exploiting the engine of C4 photosynthesis. J Exp Bot. 2011;62:29893000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33287e829">7</a>]. Enhanced photosynthetic efficiency associated with C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis not only increases productivity (i.e. grain or biomass yield), but also nutrient and water use efficiency [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sage RF, Sage TL, Kocacinar F. Photorespiration and the evolution of C4 photosynthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2012;63:1947." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33287e834">8</a>]. These benefits are the result of a carbon concentrating mechanism (CCM) that evolved to increases the CO<sub>2</sub> concentration around the carboxylating enzyme ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase (Rubisco). Concentrating CO<sub>2</sub> around Rubisco reduces its oxygenase activity, thereby significantly decreasing the amount of energy lost to photorespiration. The CCM of the majority of C<sub>4</sub> species is achieved through partitioning of the biochemical reactions of photosynthesis into two cell types (mesophyll, M, and bundle sheath, BS) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sage RF. The evolution of C4 photosynthesis. New Phytol. 2004;161:34170." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33287e844">9</a>].</p><p>Despite being a complex trait, C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis has independently evolved over 60 times in the angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Sage RF, Christin PA, Edwards EJ. The C4 plant lineages of planet Earth. J Exp Bot. 2011;62:315569." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d33287e852">10</a>] and at least 22 times in the grasses [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e855">11</a>]. The grasses are one of the most ecologically and economically significant plant clades and thus insights into the origins of C<sub>4</sub> should provide opportunities for breeding and engineering improved germplasm. However, to date, comparative genomic approaches to studying the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis have been limited to comparisons between crop species. These include C<sub>4</sub> crops such as <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Setaria italica</i>, and <i>Saccharum officinarum</i> from the clade containing the subfamilies Panicoideae, Arundinoideae, Chloridoideae, Micrairoideae, Aristidoideae, and Danthonioideae (PACMAD) and C<sub>3</sub> crops such as <i>Oryza sativa</i> and <i>Triticum aestivum</i> from the clade containing the subfamilies Bambusoideae, Ehrhartoideae, and Pooideae (BEP). The limitations of PACMAD-BEP comparisons are that these two groups of grasses diverged more than 50 million years ago and the BEP clade contains no C<sub>4</sub> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e888">11</a>].</p><p>Distant evolutionary relationships sometimes fail to identify the genomic changes associated with the evolutionary emergence of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis because differences in the photosynthetic pathway are confounded with the many other changes that occurred in the long independent history of the two lineages. The use of PACMAD-BEP comparisons has been driven by the availability of genomic resources. Currently the only published panicoid genome sequences are for panicoid species that utilize the C4 pathway for carbon fixation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schnable PS, Ware D, Fulton RS, Stein JC, Wei F, Pasternak S, et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science. 2009;326:11125." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d33287e896">12</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Cannarozzi G, Plaza-Wuthrich S, Esfeld K, Larti S, Wilson YS, Girma D, et al. Genome and transcriptome sequencing identifies breeding targets in the orphan crop tef (Eragrostis tef). BMC Genomics. 2014;15:581." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33287e899">15</a>].</p><p>
                        <i>Dichanthelium oligosanthes</i> is a C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass and thus an excellent species for comparisons to C<sub>4</sub> panicoids such as <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, and <i>S. officinarum</i>, and species with an independent C<sub>4</sub> origin represented by <i>S. italica</i>, <i>Cenchrus americanus</i>, <i>Panicum miliaceum</i>, and <i>Panicum virgatum</i> (see Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig1">1</a>). Within the genus <i>Dichanthelium</i>, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is reported to be diploid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gould FW. Chromosome numbers of southwest gasses. Am J Bot. 1958;45:75767." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d33287e947">16</a>] and is widely distributed across North America (USDA, National Resource Conservation Service), increasing the accessibility of diverse germplasm and its utility in studying adaptation to abiotic stresses. Recently <i>D. clandestinum</i> was utilized in a comparative RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) experiment [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Brautigam A, Schliesky S, Kulahoglu C, Osborne CP, Weber AP. Towards an integrative model of C4 photosynthetic subtypes: insights from comparative transcriptome analysis of NAD-ME, NADP-ME, and PEP-CK C4 species. J Exp Bot. 2014;65:357993." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33287e953">17</a>]. However, while transcriptomics data are useful, they do not provide key structural information (such as promoter and regulatory sequences) or evidence of orthology through syntenic relationships.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>
                                    <i>D. oligosanthes</i> a C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass. A greenhouse grown <i>D. oligosanthes</i> plant (<b>a</b>), and panicle (<b>b</b>). <b>c</b> Bayesian tree of three chloroplast loci, showing the monophyly of <i>Dichanthelium</i> and placement of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>. Bayesian posterior probability values above branches, maximum likelihood bootstrap below; heavy branches received maximal support in both analyses (1.0, 100, respectively). The branch marked with * was resolved differently in the ML analysis but with low support. C<sub>4</sub> clades are in <i>green shaded boxes</i>. The two major clades of grasses, PACMAD and BEP, are indicated by <i>brackets</i> as is subfamily Panicoideae</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                     <p>As the number of sequenced genomes increases, a more comprehensive understanding of the genes involved in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis can be achieved. To this end, we sequenced and assembled a draft genome of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>. Histological, biochemical, and transcriptomic analyses confirm the C<sub>3</sub> nature of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and demonstrate its usefulness as a C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass for evolutionary comparisons. Furthermore, characteristics of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> also make it a potentially suitable genetic model for dissecting traits such as perenniality, cold tolerance, and flowering time. We demonstrate here how this high quality draft genome provides novel insights into the evolution and diversification of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis in the grasses.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Life history and phylogeny</h3><p>The genus <i>Dichanthelium</i> includes ca. 72 species, which collectively are known as rosette grasses [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Freckmann RW, Lelong MG. Dichanthelium. In: Barkworth ME, editor. Flora of North America, vol. 25. New York: Oxford University Press; 2003." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1044">18</a>]. All species are perennial and plants overwinter as a rosette that grows to produce sparsely branched culms in the spring (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig1">1</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Freckmann RW, Lelong MG. Dichanthelium. In: Barkworth ME, editor. Flora of North America, vol. 25. New York: Oxford University Press; 2003." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1050">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mohlenbrock RH. Vascular flora of Illinois. Carbondale: SIU Press; 2002." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1053">19</a>] (AJS and EAK, personal observations). In many species the rosette leaves senesce late in the growing season, the culms develop more branches, and a second round of flowering occurshence the genus name, which means twice-flowering. Cleistogamy is common in <i>Dichanthelium</i> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Freckmann RW, Lelong MG. Dichanthelium. In: Barkworth ME, editor. Flora of North America, vol. 25. New York: Oxford University Press; 2003." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1060">18</a>]. While some of the inflorescences are borne well above the leaf sheath, others, particularly those from culm branches, never fully exert and self-pollinate without opening [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Freckmann RW, Lelong MG. Dichanthelium. In: Barkworth ME, editor. Flora of North America, vol. 25. New York: Oxford University Press; 2003." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1063">18</a>] (EAK, personal observations).</p><p>
                           <i>Dichanthelium</i> is a member of the grass subfamily Panicoideae, tribe Paniceae. Like other members of Panicoideae, it has spikelets with two flowers, the upper one bisexual and the lower staminate or sterile (EAK, personal observations). Like other members of the tribe Paniceae, its chromosomes are in multiples of 9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gould FW. Chromosome numbers of southwest gasses. Am J Bot. 1958;45:75767." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1072">16</a>]. Species of <i>Dichanthelium</i> are similar to but morphologically distinct from species of <i>Panicum</i>, so for many years <i>Dichanthelium</i> was treated as a subgenus of <i>Panicum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Clayton WD, Renvoize SA. Genera graminum. Grasses of the world. Kew Bulletin Additional Series. 1986;13:1389." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1088">20</a>], a treatment that is still followed by some authors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Clayton WD, Harman KT, Williamson H. GrassBase - The online world grass flora. 2006 onwards. &#xA;                    http://www.kew.org/data/grasses-db.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1091">21</a>]. However, it was recognized as a distinct genus by Gould and Clark in 1978 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gould FW, Clark CA. Dichanthelium (Poaceae) in the United States and Canada. Ann Missouri Bot Gard. 1978;65:1088132." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1094">22</a>].</p><p>Phylogenetic data support the distinction of <i>Dichanthelium</i> as a separate genus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1103">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Giussani LM, Cota-Sanchez JH, Zuloaga FO, Kellogg EA. A Molecular phylogeny of the grass subfamily Panicoideae (Poaceae) shows multiple origins of C4 photosynthesis. Am J Bot. 2001;88:19932012." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1106">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Aliscioni SS, Giussani LM, Zuloaga FO, Kellogg EA. A Molecular phylogeny of Panicum (Poaceae: Paniceae): tests of monophyly and phylogenetic placement within the Panicoideae. Am J Bot. 2003;90:796821." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1109">24</a>], showing that it is clearly not a lineage in the <i>Panicum</i> clade. Within the tribe Paniceae, the position of <i>Dichanthelium</i> is uncertain. Chloroplast sequences show that it is closely related to the large clade (the MPC clade) that includes groups of species utilizing different C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis subtypes: <i>Melinidinae</i> (PEPCK), <i>Panicinae</i> (NAD-ME), and <i>Cenchrinae</i> (NADP-ME) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Washburn JD, Schnable JC, Davidse G, Pires JC. Phylogeny and photosynthesis of the grass tribe Paniceae. Amer J Bot. 2015;102:1493505." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1130">25</a>]. Depending on the sample of taxa and chloroplast sequences, <i>Dichanthelium</i> is either sister to the MPC plus the Australian species <i>Homopholis</i> and <i>Walwhalleya</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1143">11</a>] or as part of a larger clade including mostly C<sub>3</sub> species but also the C<sub>4</sub> members of the Australian Neurachninae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Washburn JD, Schnable JC, Davidse G, Pires JC. Phylogeny and photosynthesis of the grass tribe Paniceae. Amer J Bot. 2015;102:1493505." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1151">25</a>]. In contrast, data from a single nuclear gene sequence place it sister to all Paniceae except <i>Echinochloa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Vicentini A, Barber JC, Aliscioni SS, Giussani LM, Kellogg EA. The age of the grasses and clusters of origins of C4 photosynthesis. Glob Chang Biol. 2008;14:296377." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1157">26</a>]. In either case, it is more closely related to the C<sub>4</sub> panicoid species than any of the C<sub>3</sub> grasses for which complete genome assemblies are currently available (e.g. <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i>). The close relationship between <i>Dichanthelium</i> and C<sub>4</sub> Paniceae is confirmed when sequences from the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> chloroplast genes <i>rbcL</i>, <i>ndhF</i>, and <i>matK</i> were used in maximum likelihood and Bayesian analyses with select species previously used to construct a phylogeny of the grasses (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig1">1c</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1192">11</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Anatomy and physiology of <i>Dichanthelium oligosanthes</i>
                        </h3><p>Histological analysis of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> leaf cross-sections indicates that its anatomy is consistent with that of a temperate C<sub>3</sub> grass. Vacuoles occupy the majority of the cell volume in M cells, with chloroplasts arranged near the cell periphery (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig2">2</a>). Large air spaces are present between loosely arranged spongy mesophyll cells. The BS cells of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> have few and small chloroplasts, a hallmark of C<sub>3</sub> species (<i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> cross-sections can be found in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="von Caemmerer S, Quick WP, Furbank RT. The development of C4 rice: current progress and future challenges. Science. 2012;336:16712." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1227">27</a>]). The altered cellular arrangement, known as Kranz anatomy, that facilitates the CCM is clearly absent from the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> leaf cross-section. While many variations of Kranz anatomy have been identified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Brown WV. Variations in anatomy, associations, and origins of Kranz tissue. Amer J Bot. 1975;62:395402." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1233">28</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hattersley PW, Watson L. Diversification of photosynthesis. In: Chapman GP, editor. Grass evolution and domestication. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press; 1992." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1236">31</a>], generally C<sub>4</sub> species have narrow vein spacing, with large BS cells arranged around the vasculature and M cells surrounding the BS cells [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hattersley PW, Watson L. Anatomical parameters for predicting photosynthetic pathways of grass leaves: The maximum lateral cell count and the maximum cells distant count. Phytomorphol. 1975;25:32533." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1242">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Brown WV. The Kranz syndrome and its subtypes in grass systematics. Mem Torrey Bot Club. 1977;23:197." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1245">33</a>]. In C<sub>4</sub> Panicoideae species, typically two M cells separate consecutive BS (BS-M-M-BS), but <i>D. oligosanthes</i> has many mesophyll cells between veins, which is consistent with C<sub>3</sub> leaf anatomy (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i> leaf <i>cross-section</i>. Toluidine blue-stained leaf cross-section shows stained chloroplasts present in mesophyll cells (M) but generally lacking in bundle sheath cells (BS). Wide vein spacing is apparent in the leaf, which is characteristic of a C<sub>3</sub> species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>The CO<sub>2</sub> response curve generated from <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is also typical of a C<sub>3</sub> species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig3">3</a>). The data fit well with the C<sub>3</sub> model of photosynthesis, characterized by a higher compensation point (approximately 48.2 bar) and a more gradual increase in photosynthesis at low <i>p</i>CO<sub>2</sub> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="von Caemmerer S. Biochemical models of leaf photosynthesis. Collingwood: CSIRO Publishing; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1307">34</a>]. The initial slope of the response curve is typical of C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis whereas a much steeper slope is observed in C<sub>4</sub> species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i> leaf gas exchange. Net CO<sub>2</sub> assimilation in response to changes in intercellular CO<sub>2</sub>. <i>Squares</i> correspond to gas exchange data from <i>D. oligosanthes. Lines</i> represent modeled AC<sub>i</sub> curves in C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Biochemical assays revealed typical activity levels of Rubisco, phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC), and carbonic anhydrase (CA) for a C<sub>3</sub> monocot species (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Tab1">1</a>). The measured in vitro Rubisco activity corresponds well with V<sub>cmax</sub> values estimated from gas exchange and is consistent with other C<sub>3</sub> species. PEPC activity is low, as predicted by RNA-seq data and as expected for a C<sub>3</sub> species where it likely functions in an anaplerotic role for TCA cycle activities. CA activity is within the broad range of CA activity levels in C<sub>3</sub> grass species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Gillon J, Yakir D. Influence of carbonic anhydrase activity in terrestrial vegetation on the 18O content of atmospheric CO2. Science. 2001;291:25847." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1370">35</a>]. The plants measured here show high variation in total Rubisco and CA activity likely due to differences in plant maturity; however, all measured values are within the accepted ranges for C<sub>3</sub> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Gillon J, Yakir D. Influence of carbonic anhydrase activity in terrestrial vegetation on the 18O content of atmospheric CO2. Science. 2001;291:25847." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1375">35</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Svensson P, Blasing OE, Westhoff P. Evolution of C4 phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase. Arch Biochem Biophys. 2003;414:1808." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1378">37</a>].</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i> physiology and biochemical characteristics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>
                           <i>D. oligosanthes</i> has a distinctively C<sub>3</sub> isotopic signature (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Tab1">1</a>; [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="OLeary M. Carbon isotope fractionation in plants. Phytochemistry. 1981;21:55367." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1634">38</a>]), which is consistent with other <i>Dichanthelium</i> species that have been previously reported [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Brautigam A, Schliesky S, Kulahoglu C, Osborne CP, Weber AP. Towards an integrative model of C4 photosynthetic subtypes: insights from comparative transcriptome analysis of NAD-ME, NADP-ME, and PEP-CK C4 species. J Exp Bot. 2014;65:357993." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1641">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smith BN, Brown WV. The Kranz syndrome in the Gramineae as indicated by carbon isotopic ratios. Am J Bot. 1973;60:50513." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1644">39</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Brown WV, Smith BN. The genus Dichanthelium (Gramineae). Bull Torrey Bot Club. 1975;102:103." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1647">40</a>]. This value reflects strong isotopic discrimination by Rubisco, demonstrating that CO<sub>2</sub> fixation occurs via the C<sub>3</sub> cycle. Both C<sub>4</sub> species and Type II C<sub>3</sub>-C<sub>4</sub> intermediates have distinct isotopic signatures indicating CO<sub>2</sub> fixed by PEPC. However, type I intermediates have a C<sub>3</sub>-like isotopic signature, but can be differentiated by anatomical and biochemical characteristics that are more similar to C<sub>4</sub> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sage RF. The evolution of C4 photosynthesis. New Phytol. 2004;161:34170." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1667">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Edwards GE, Ku MSB. The biochemistry of C3-C4 intermediates. In: Hatch MD, Boardman K, editors. The Biochemistry of Plants, vol. 14. New York: Academic Press; 1987. p. 275325." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1671">41</a>]. Taken together, the leaf anatomy, gas exchange, and biochemical measurements corroborate previous reports that <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is a C<sub>3</sub> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Moss DN, Krenzer EG, Brun WA. Carbon dioxide compensation points in related plant species. Science. 1969;164:1878." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1679">42</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Nuclear and chloroplast genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>To estimate the genome size of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, flow cytometry and k-mer abundance assays were performed. Flow cytometry of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> accession <i>Kellogg 1175</i> produced an estimated genome size of approximately 960 Mb, placing it within the range for diploid panicoid grasses. Single copy sequences present a distinctive peak in histograms of k-mer abundance centered at the average depth of sequencing. Sequences repeated twice in the genome form a second peak at twice the average sequencing depth and so on. Based on k-mer analysis, the estimated genome size of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> was revised downward to 750 Mb of which approximately 360 Mb is single copy sequence.</p><p>Sequence analysis was performed on a single individual derived from self-pollination of a wild-collected individual (<i>Kellogg 1175</i>), collected at the Shaw Nature Reserve in Gray Summit, MO. <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is a predominately self-pollinating species, so heterozygosity was expected to be low. A <i>D. oligosanthes</i> draft assembly was generated using data from libraries with median 180 bp insert and 5 kb insert sizes. Sequencing was performed on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform with 100 bp paired end sequencing. Approximately 90 Gb and 86 Gb of sequence was generated from the 180 bp and 5 kb libraries, respectively. These data were assembled using Allpaths-LG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:15138." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1714">43</a>]. Additional scaffolding was conducted using two mate pair insert libraries (5 kb median and 6.3 kb median insert size) and the software package SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:5789." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1717">44</a>] and sequence present in a number of gaps was determined using GapCloster [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience. 2012;1:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1721">45</a>]. The final assembly consisted of 17,441 scaffolds (589 megabases), which were constructed from 76,905 contigs (476 megabases of sequence). The assembly we present here therefore covers 78 % of the estimated total genome of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, including determined sequence for 63 % of the genome. Based on the alignment of a set of low copy genes conserved in <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>S. italica</i>, and <i>Panicum hallii</i>, we determined that our assembly contains at least 98 % of the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> gene space (3358/3430 genes identified). A total of 30,153 genes were annotated through a combination of homology-based and de novo annotation using Maker2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1740">46</a>]. For these genes, 1 kb of promoter sequence was recovered 94.2 % of the time, and 5 kb of promoter sequence was recovered 80.5 % of the time. 86.5 % of all annotated genes were present on multi-gene scaffolds, enabling syntenic comparisons to other grass genomes.</p><p>The resulting assemblies and gene model annotations were loaded into CoGe to explore synteny relationships and enable community access to the datasets through iPlant servers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lyons E, Freeling M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 2008;53:66173." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1746">47</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Alam M, Ming R, Tang H, et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 2008;148:177281." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1749">48</a>]. Through the use of CoGes syntenic path assembly algorithm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Lyons E, Freeling M, Kustu S, Inwood W. Using genomic sequencing for classical genetics in E. coli K12. Plos One. 2011;6:e16717." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1752">49</a>], the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> scaffolds were compared to the genomes of <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. italica</i> to show the overall coverage of the genome (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig4">4</a>). Coverage of the euchromatic arms is quite good, with most of the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> genome present in large enough scaffolds to be ordered and oriented based on syntenic data from close relatives. Centromeric and pericentromeric regions are not well represented in the syntenic path assemblies. This may be because pericentromeric regions are highly repetitive and are more difficult to assemble, and/or because pericentromeric regions tend to have lower gene content and fewer conserved genes between species. No evidence of significant gene-loss was identified when comparing syntenic orthologous regions of these three genomes, which confirms that this reference accession of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, like <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. italica</i>, and unlike <i>Z. mays</i>, is diploid relative to the common ancestor of the grasses.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i> genome synteny with <i>S. italica</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>. Syntenic path assemblies comparing the content of the <i>Dichanthelium oligosanthes</i> draft genome assembly to the two most closely related species with pseudomolecule level assemblies. <b>a</b> Comparison to <i>S. italica</i>. <b>b</b> Comparison to <i>S. bicolor</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>In addition to the nuclear genome assembly, a subset of the data was used to create a de novo assembly of the chloroplast genome. An initial assembly was made using SPAdes v.3.1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bankevich A, Nurk S, Antipov D, Gurevich AA, Dvorkin M, Kulikov AS, et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J Comput Biol. 2012;19:45577." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1831">50</a>] and the resulting contigs were further assembled using Sequencher (Genecodes 5.2.4). Gaps between contigs were filled using raw sequencing reads to extend the contig ends (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Sec12">Methods</a>). The overlapping regions of the contigs were then verified by mapping reads to the junction in Sequencher. Read depth across the assembled chloroplast sequence was checked by estimating 20-mer abundance using Jellyfish v.2.1.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Marcais G, Kingsford C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:76470." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1837">51</a>] and mapping abundance to 20-mers across the assembly. No breaks or large shifts in coverage, other than what is expected at inverted repeat boundaries, were identified (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Tab2">2</a>). Annotation of the chloroplast genome was performed using Dual Organellar GenoMe Annotator (DOGMA) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wyman SK, Jansen RK, Boore JL. Automatic annotation of organellar genomes with DOGMA. Bioinformatics. 2004;20:32525." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1843">52</a>] and visualized with Circos [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19:163945." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1847">53</a>] (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). No large-scale rearrangements or duplications were identified in the plastid genome of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> relative to other panicoid grass plastomes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i> chloroplast genome assembly statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative analysis of genes expression across leaf development</h3><p>One of the unique features of monocot leaves is that developmental processes proceed linearly, with the base segments being the least and the tip being the most differentiated [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li P, Ponnala L, Gandotra N, Wang L, Si Y, Tausta SL, et al. The developmental dynamics of the maize leaf transcriptome. Nat Genet. 2010;42:10607." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1954">54</a>]. This continuous gradient has been exploited previously to investigate the expression of genes related to photosynthesis in <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1963">55</a>] and <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ding Z, Weissmann S, Wang M, Du B, Huang L, Wang L, et al. Identification of photosynthesis-associated C4 candidate genes through comparative leaf gradient transcriptome in multiple lineages of C3 and C4 species. Plos One. 2015;10:e0140629." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1973">56</a>]. As a C<sub>3</sub> panicoid grass, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> provides a unique opportunity to examine the diversification of genes and networks associated with C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis using comparative transcriptomic approaches. To expand the gradient analyses, developmental leaf gradients were constructed for <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and the closely related C<sub>4</sub> species <i>S. viridis</i>. The <i>S. viridis</i> gradient from [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ding Z, Weissmann S, Wang M, Du B, Huang L, Wang L, et al. Identification of photosynthesis-associated C4 candidate genes through comparative leaf gradient transcriptome in multiple lineages of C3 and C4 species. Plos One. 2015;10:e0140629." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33287e1995">56</a>] was not used because the data were not replicated.</p><p>The same growth conditions employed in previously published grass leaf gradients were used for <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> to reduce environmental variation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2007">55</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ding Z, Weissmann S, Wang M, Du B, Huang L, Wang L, et al. Identification of photosynthesis-associated C4 candidate genes through comparative leaf gradient transcriptome in multiple lineages of C3 and C4 species. Plos One. 2015;10:e0140629." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2010">56</a>]. Leaf gradients were generated by collecting four segments from the third leaf from each species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig5">5a and b</a>). While leaf length varies among species, the developmental programs that establish the anatomy and biochemistry for photosynthesis proceed in the same direction and segments of the third leaf above the point at which it is enveloped by the sheath of the second leaf are expected to act as source tissues while those below remain sink tissues. The <i>S. viridis</i> leaves were sampled similarly to a previously published four segment gradient of <i>Z. mays</i>, which captured the basal, transition, maturing, and mature zones of the leaf [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li P, Ponnala L, Gandotra N, Wang L, Si Y, Tausta SL, et al. The developmental dynamics of the maize leaf transcriptome. Nat Genet. 2010;42:10607." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2023">54</a>]. Because the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> third leaf is small, only a single segment could be collected below the second leaf ligule, whereas two segments were collected in <i>S. viridis</i>. Thus segment 1 of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> captures the equivalent of both base and transition (segments 1 and 2) of <i>S. viridis</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig5">5a and b</a>). The <i>D. oligosanthes</i> leaf above the ligule was divided into three equal segments. Leaf segments 1, 3, 7, and 12 of <i>S. bicolor</i> were used from [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ding Z, Weissmann S, Wang M, Du B, Huang L, Wang L, et al. Identification of photosynthesis-associated C4 candidate genes through comparative leaf gradient transcriptome in multiple lineages of C3 and C4 species. Plos One. 2015;10:e0140629." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2048">56</a>] in the analyses because they are most similar to the segments collected in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and <i>S. viridis</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Leaf developmental gradients. The third leaf of <b>a</b> 
                                       <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, <b>b</b> 
                                       <i>S. viridis</i>, and <b>c</b> 
                                       <i>S. bicolor. Shaded boxes</i> indicate the portion of the leaf shaded by the second leaf ligule. <i>Dotted lines</i> indicate the segments sampled for the expression analyses. <b>d</b> 
                                       <i>Heatmap</i> showing a Spearman correlation of the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> replicated leaf segment RNA-seq data</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Hierarchical clustering of global gene expression profiles using Spearman correlation values (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S2) indicates that the replicates of each segment are strongly correlated and each segment clusters separately (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig5">5d</a>). Pearson correlation analysis produced similar results. The strong correlation between segments 3 and 4 suggests that the tip of the leaf may be fully mature. These new leaf gradients provide an opportunity to investigate a variety of biological processes, including changes in gene regulation linked to the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">C<sub>4</sub> carbon shuttle gene expression</h3><p>It is not surprising that all of the major enzymes involved in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis are present in C<sub>3</sub> species given that C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis has evolved from the C<sub>3</sub> ancestral state over 70 times in the angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Sage RF, Sage TL, Kocacinar F. Photorespiration and the evolution of C4 photosynthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2012;63:1947." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2139">8</a>]. Increased gene expression of the core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes plays a major role in the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis (reviewed in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sage RF. The evolution of C4 photosynthesis. New Phytol. 2004;161:34170." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2147">9</a>]). To investigate the primary method of carbon fixation for <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, we compared the expression of six core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> to its C<sub>4</sub> relatives <i>S. viridis</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig6">6</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S1).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig6_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig6_HTML.gif" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Expression profiles of core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes. Expression profiles of six core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes in <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, <i>S. viridis</i>, and <i>S. bicolor</i>. Expression values in FPKM are shown on the <i>y-axis</i> and the leaf segment is indicated on the <i>x-axis</i>. Transcriptional upregulation of these enzymes is not observed in <i>D. oligosanthes</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Consistent with the results of the anatomical and physiological analyses, the expression profile of all six core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes indicates that <i>D. oligosanthes</i> utilizes the ancestral C<sub>3</sub> carbon fixation pathway. Large amounts of CA protein are known to be present in the leaves of C<sub>3</sub> plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Okabe K, Yang S, Tsuziki M, Miyachi S. Carbonic anhydrase: Its content in spinach leaves and its taxonomic diversity studied with anti-spinach leaf carbonic anhydrase antibody. Plant Sci Lett. 1984;33:14553." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2227">57</a>] and of the six genes encoding enzymes in the C<sub>4</sub> pathway, only <i>carbonic anhydrase1</i> (<i>ca1</i>) was expressed at a high level in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig6">6</a>). Significant accumulation of transcripts encoded by four other C<sub>4</sub> genes was observed in <i>S. viridis</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>. Expression levels of the C<sub>4</sub> genes were similar in <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> except for <i>ca1</i> and <i>nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate malic enzyme</i> (<i>nadp-me</i>), both of which are twofold lower in <i>S. bicolor</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig6">6</a>). Although comparing absolute expression levels across species introduces numerous potential sources of bias and error, a difference in the number of tandemly duplicated <i>ca</i> gene copies likely explains the expression difference between <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. viridis</i>. Unlike <i>ca</i>, <i>nadp-me</i> does not have highly expressed paralogs. Protein blot and enzyme activity assays would be needed to determine whether differences in enzymatic efficiency and/or differences in translational regulation compensate for the difference in transcriptional abundance for this gene.</p><p>Three major subtypes of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis are recognized as (1) NADP-ME, (2) NAD-ME, and (3) PEPCK, named for the primary decarboxylating enzyme employed by each subtype. While traditionally these pathways have been viewed as independent, biochemical data and recent modeling of the C<sub>4</sub> pathways revealed that the PEPCK pathway could be complementary in NADP-ME subtype species, such as <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>S. viridis</i>, and <i>S. bicolor</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang Y, Brautigam A, Weber AP, Zhu XG. Three distinct biochemical subtypes of C4 photosynthesis? A modelling analysis. J Exp Bot. 2014;65:356778." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2311">58</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Furbank RT. Evolution of the C4 photosynthetic mechanism: are there really three C4 acid decarboxylation types? J Exp Bot. 2011;62:31038." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2314">59</a>]. Accordingly, although <i>Z. mays</i> is classified as an NADP-ME C<sub>4</sub> subtype, the PEPCK pathway is likely active and contributes to total photosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Hatch MD. The C4-pathway of photosynthesis. Evidence for an intermediate pool of carbon dioxide and the identity of the donor C4-dicarboxylic acid. Biochem J. 1971;125:42532." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2322">60</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Wingler A, Walker RP, Chen Z-H, Leegood RC. Phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase is involved in the decarboxylation of aspartate in the bundle sheath of maize. Plant Physiol. 1999;120:53945." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2325">61</a>]. This is reflected in the expression of <i>pepck1</i> in <i>Z. mays</i>, which accumulates to 2000 rpkm in the developing leaf tip and follows the expected expression profile of a gene involved in photosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2335">55</a>]. Interestingly, very low <i>pepck</i> expression levels were observed in both <i>S. viridis</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig6">6</a>). The reported lack of PEPCK protein in <i>S. bicolor</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Koteyeva NK, Voznesenskaya EV, Edwards GE. An assessment of the capacity for phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase to contribute to C4 photosynthesis. Plant Sci. 2015;235:7080." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2354">62</a>] confirms the expression result and provides further evidence that the PEPCK pathway is not active in these species. <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> are believed to share a recent common ancestor [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2363">11</a>], suggesting the acquisition of the PEPCK pathway in <i>Z. mays</i> may be a relatively new evolutionary innovation or, alternatively, that this secondary pathway was lost in <i>S. bicolor</i> after it diverged from <i>Z. mays</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">C<sub>4</sub> transcription factor identification</h3><p>The use of a single pairwise comparison between distantly related C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> species to identify genes linked to C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis is likely to produce large numbers of false positives. Any C<sub>4</sub> panicoid differs from C<sub>3</sub> BEP species in many aspects unrelated to the evolution of photosynthetic pathway. For example, a comparison of <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> leaves will identify gene expression differences that have accrued in the intervening 100 million years (50 million years on each branch since the common ancestor).
