Thus, there will have been fewer mutations between <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>D. oligosanthes</i> because the intervening time is 30 million years and fewer changes to be confounded with differences between C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub>. Multiple comparisons between species from two independent origins of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis using a close C<sub>3</sub> species for comparison can also distinguish changes that have occurred between the two C<sub>4</sub> origins and are not directly related to C<sub>4</sub>. Changes that map to the <i>S. viridis</i> branch alone are specific to that lineage, whereas changes that appear independently on both the <i>S. viridis</i> and Andropogoneae branches are likely to reflect instances in which C<sub>4</sub> has converged to use common genes. This would produce a shorter list of higher confidence gene candidates, ideally to the point where it would be practical to pursue functional validation of each individual gene. The leaf gradients of <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, and <i>S. viridis</i> allow us to produce such a list. A previous study of a developmental leaf gradient in <i>Z. mays</i> identified three clusters of co-expressed genes correlated with photosynthetic activity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2444">55</a>]. These three clusters include 82 of the 1286 total transcription factors (TFs) annotated and expressed in the <i>Z. mays</i> leaf, 55 of which have an average expression across the leaf of at least 4 FPKM. Because general expression patterns were being compared across several species, this criterion was necessary for robust comparisons. Using the data from the leaf gradients reported here, an additional filter was imposed to restrict the list to only those TFs that showed a different expression profile in the leaves of <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, and/or <i>S. viridis</i> when compared to <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>. Only eight TFs met these criteria (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig7">7</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM3">3</a>: Table S2). Three of the eight TFs identified here are common to the 118 C<sub>4</sub> TFs described by Wang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2475">55</a>] (GRMZM2G130149, GRMZM2G061906, GRMZM2G119999), but none of these TFs were identified in a comparison between <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>Cleome gynandra</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Aubry S, Kelly S, Kmpers BM, Smith-Unna RD, Hibberd JM. Deep evolutionary comparison of gene expression identifies parallel recruitment of trans-factors in two independent origins of C4 photosynthesis. Plos One. 2014;10:e1004365." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2485">63</a>]. Four of the eight TFs displayed unique expression profiles in <i>Z. mays</i> (GRMZM2G147152, GRMZM2G040481, GRMZM2G098986, GRMZM2G130149). One of these (GRMZM2G098986) does not have an ortholog in the other species and is incorporated into a LTR transposable element. Three TFs in <i>Z. mays</i> share an expression profile with <i>S. bicolor</i> but not <i>S. viridis</i>, <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, or <i>Oryza sativa</i> (GRMZM2G119999, GRMZM2G061906, GRMZM2G054252). This result suggests that modified regulation of these TFs is specific to the Andropogoneae tribe rather than diagnostic of C<sub>4</sub> function. A single TF shows a similar profile with all C<sub>4</sub> panicoid species, but neither of the C<sub>3</sub> species (GRMZM2G140355).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig7_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig7_HTML.gif" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Putative C<sub>4</sub> transcription factors. Expression profiles for each of the transcription factors identified in the comparative analysis. Expression values in FPKM are shown on the <i>y-axis</i> and the leaf segment is indicated on the <i>x-axis. Colored lines</i> represent each species as follows: <i>black</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>; <i>red</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>; <i>blue</i>, <i>S. viridis</i>; <i>green</i>, <i>D. oligosanthes</i>; and <i>purple</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>. Leaf <i>cross-section diagrams</i> are <i>colored</i> to show the expression of each TF in M and BS cells of <i>Z. mays</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li P, Ponnala L, Gandotra N, Wang L, Si Y, Tausta SL, et al. The developmental dynamics of the maize leaf transcriptome. Nat Genet. 2010;42:10607." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2572">54</a>]. Higher expression values are shown as <i>red</i>, while lower expression values are shown as <i>yellow</i>
                                    </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>The four TFs identified that have expression profiles specific to <i>Z. mays</i> most likely result from the use of the <i>Z. mays</i> leaf developmental gradient as the initial filtering step and do not reflect a difference in the number of TFs with lineage-specific gene expression patterns among the species included in this analysis. However, given that the PEPCK pathway is specific to <i>Z. mays</i> in this small sample of C<sub>4</sub> species, it is tempting to speculate that the <i>Z. mays</i> specific TFs contribute to regulation of the PEPCK pathway. Interestingly, utilizing data on cell type specific expression from the <i>Z. mays</i> eFP browser (<a href="http://bar.utoronto.ca/efp_maize/cgi-bin/efpWeb.cgi">http://bar.utoronto.ca/efp_maize/cgi-bin/efpWeb.cgi</a>), a single TF was identified as preferentially expressed in bundle sheath cells, where PEPCK is needed for C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis. Taken together, these results suggest that the myb TF encoded by GRMZM2G130149 may be one of the genes that regulate the transcription of <i>pepck</i> in <i>Z. mays</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">C<sub>4</sub> specific amino acids under selection</h3><p>In addition to probing the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis using expression data, we also investigated amino acid substitutions in key C<sub>4</sub> enzymes. Specific residues in several of the C<sub>4</sub> carbon shuttle genes have been previously shown to be under positive selection in C<sub>4</sub> lineages. These include PEPC [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Christin PA, Salamin N, Savolainen V, Duvall MR, Besnard G. C4 Photosynthesis evolved in grasses via parallel adaptive genetic changes. Curr Biol. 2007;17:12417." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2646">4</a>], which encodes the first carboxylation reaction in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, the decarboxylating enzymes NADP-ME [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Christin PA, Samaritani E, Petitpierre B, Salamin N, Besnard G. Evolutionary insights on C4 photosynthetic subtypes in grasses from genomics and phylogenetics. Genome Biol Evol. 2009;1:22130." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2652">64</a>] and PEPCK [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Christin PA, Petitpierre B, Salamin N, Buchi L, Besnard G. Evolution of C4 phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase in grasses, from genotype to phenotype. Mol Biol Evol. 2009;26:35765." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2655">65</a>], as well as the large subunit of Rubisco (rbcL) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Christin PA, Salamin N, Muasya AM, Roalson EH, Russier F, Besnard G. Evolutionary switch and genetic convergence on rbcL following the evolution of C4 photosynthesis. Mol Biol Evol. 2008;25:23618." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2658">66</a>]. Peptide sequences for these genes in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> were compared to the known amino acid sequences across the grass family to identify signatures of selection in the amino acid sequences prior to the divergence of the C<sub>4</sub> lineages from <i>D. oligosanthes</i>.</p><p>Despite having sister taxa that evolved all three sub-types of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> does not contain the key amino acid substitutions present in the C<sub>4</sub> enzymes. The most studied of these is the A780S substitution in PEPC. C<sub>4</sub> lineages that have evolved the serine substitution are not inhibited by malate and require a lower PEP substrate concentration, which is advantageous for the C<sub>4</sub>, but not the C<sub>3</sub> pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Svensson P, Blasing OE, Westhoff P. Evolution of C4 phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase. Arch Biochem Biophys. 2003;414:1808." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2687">37</a>]. While other amino acids in PEPC are under positive selection in C<sub>4</sub> species, the A780S substitution is common to most C<sub>4</sub> lineages and is absent in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig8">8a</a>). A maximum-likelihood tree was used to ensure that the correct <i>D. oligosanthes</i> PEPC isoform was used for the analysis (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S3, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM4">4</a>: PEPC Amino Acid Sequences). Likewise, although most C<sub>4</sub> species contain only a subset of the amino acids predominantly found among all C<sub>4</sub> species, none of the prevailing C<sub>4</sub> amino acid residues were present in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> for NADP-ME, PEPCK, or rbcL. Taken together, these results suggest that the common ancestor between <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and the closely related C<sub>4</sub> species at the point of divergence was not under selective pressure for C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis or had not yet acquired mutations to develop C<sub>4</sub>. This also strongly supports the model that C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis was acquired independently in these diverged lineages and <i>D. oligosanthes</i> does not represent a loss or reversion to the C<sub>3</sub> pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group II. New grass phylogeny resolves deep evolutionary relationships and discovers C4 origins. New Phytol. 2012;193:30412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2737">11</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig8_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig8_HTML.gif" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Amino acids under positive selection in C<sub>4</sub> lineages. (<b>a</b>) PEPC and (<b>b</b>) PEPCK amino acid sequences for C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> grass species. Amino acids are <i>color-coded</i> according to their chemical properties: <i>black</i>, non-polar; <i>red</i>, polar; <i>green</i>, acidic; <i>blue</i>, basic. C<sub>4</sub> amino acids are <i>bolded</i>. Figure modified from Christin et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Christin PA, Salamin N, Savolainen V, Duvall MR, Besnard G. C4 Photosynthesis evolved in grasses via parallel adaptive genetic changes. Curr Biol. 2007;17:12417." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2784">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Christin PA, Petitpierre B, Salamin N, Buchi L, Besnard G. Evolution of C4 phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase in grasses, from genotype to phenotype. Mol Biol Evol. 2009;26:35765." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2787">65</a>]</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Christin et al. identified a set of nine amino acid substitutions in the PEPCK gene that exhibited positive selection in species classified as the PEPCK C<sub>4</sub> subtype, such as <i>Urochloa maximus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Christin PA, Petitpierre B, Salamin N, Buchi L, Besnard G. Evolution of C4 phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase in grasses, from genotype to phenotype. Mol Biol Evol. 2009;26:35765." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2807">65</a>]. Of the two PEPCK genes present in <i>Z. mays</i>, the paralog that showed an expression pattern in our data consistent with a role in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis (PEPCK1) contains two of the nine amino acid substitutions shown to be under positive selection in C<sub>4</sub> species that utilize the PEPCK pathway. However, the copy that does not show a photosynthesis-linked pattern of expression (PEPCK2) does not contain any of the positively selected amino acid substitutions. In <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>S. viridis</i>, where no expression evidence links PEPCK to C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, none of the positively selected amino acid substitutions are present (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig8">8b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Structural evolution of the <i>carbonic anhydrase</i> gene family in the grasses</h3><p>The first biochemical step in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis is catalyzed by a beta-carbonic anhydrase (CA), which hydrates CO<sub>2</sub> to produce bicarbonate. The grass lineage contains a locus with multiple tandemly arranged <i>ca</i> genes. The <i>ca</i> genes comprising this locus in <i>Z. mays</i> were previously defined and functionally characterized in <i>Z. mays</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Studer AJ, Gandin A, Kolbe AR, Wang L, Cousins AB, Brutnell TP. A limited role for carbonic anhydrase in C4 photosynthesis as revealed by a ca1ca2 double mutant in maize. Plant Physiol. 2014;165:60817." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2858">67</a>]. Rapid Amplification of complementary DNA (cDNA) Ends (RACE) was used to define the <i>ca1</i> transcription start site in <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, <i>S. viridis</i>, and <i>S. bicolor</i>, as well as <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>. These experiments confirmed the expression of a <i>ca1</i> transcript in <i>D. oligosanthes</i> predicted to be plastid localized, which includes a ~3 kb first intron that is also present in <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>S. bicolor</i>. This long transcript predicted to encode a plastid-targeted isoform was also present, but not predicted in <i>S. viridis</i>. Transcript data from RACE experiments were used to improve the <i>ca1</i> gene annotation for <i>D. oligosanthes</i>. Furthermore, RNA-seq data revealed that <i>ca1</i> is the most highly expressed of the <i>ca</i> gene copies in both C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> species.</p><p>The number of annotated <i>ca</i> genes at this locus varies across grass species, so a comparative genomics approach was used to investigate the evolution of this tandemly arranged <i>ca</i> gene cluster (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig9">9</a>). Using BLAST and synteny comparisons available in CoGe [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lyons E, Freeling M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 2008;53:66173." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2928">47</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Alam M, Ming R, Tang H, et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 2008;148:177281." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2931">48</a>], we were able to correct misannotated <i>ca</i> genes in several species (see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM5">5</a>: Carbonic Anhydrase Coding Sequences). Most notably, the <i>ca4</i> gene in <i>O. sativa</i> was misannotated due to a large transposable element insertion into an intron (LOC_Os01g45290). Additionally, <i>Osca4</i> is truncated, lacking the last exon but still retaining the CA active site domain. The <i>ca4</i> gene was previously misannotated as a gene fragment in <i>S. bicolor</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 2009;457:5516." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33287e2957">13</a>], but was correctly annotated in the newest release of the genome (version 2.1).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig9_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-016-1080-3/MediaObjects/13059_2016_1080_Fig9_HTML.gif" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>
                                       <i>Carbonic anhydrase</i> tandem gene duplication. A syntenic region containing tandemly arranged <i>ca</i> genes in six grass species. <i>Lines</i> show the phylogenetic relationship between the species. <i>Circles</i> indicate a duplication event. <i>Arrows</i> denote gene copies and <i>white diamonds</i> indicate the enzyme active site domain. <i>Arrow colors</i> show gene orthology. Not drawn to scale</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>To enable functional comparisons between species, the orthologous/paralogous relationship of the tandemly arranged <i>ca</i> genes was examined for grass species with available genomes. <i>ca1</i> coding sequences were also obtained for <i>Panicum virgatum</i> (switchgrass), <i>Hordeum vulgare</i> (barley), <i>Triticum aestivum</i> (wheat), and <i>Musa acuminata</i> (banana). A maximum-likelihood tree of the <i>ca</i> gene copies produced distinct clades and within clades the phylogenetic relationships were preserved (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4). This analysis provides comprehensive naming of the gene copies across species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig9">9</a>).</p><p>Based on these comparative analyses, the following model describes the evolution of the <i>ca</i> gene family (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#Fig9">9</a>). The base copy number for the <i>ca</i> tandem array in the grasses is two (<i>ca1</i> and <i>ca4</i>) and in the BEP clade these two tandem gene copies have been retained without modification. In the lineage leading to the panicoid grasses, a duplication of <i>ca1</i> generated <i>ca3</i> prior to the divergence of the Andropogoneae and Paniceae lineages. The <i>ca2</i> gene copy appears to have arisen from a second duplication of <i>ca1</i> in the lineage leading to the Andropogoneae tribe and may have been linked with the duplication/fusion event that resulted in two active site domains of <i>ca1</i> for species in the same lineage. A deletion of the <i>ca4</i> gene is only observed in <i>Z. mays</i>. It is currently not clear where <i>ca4</i> is expressed or if it is functional; however, the conservation of this gene for 50 million years in both the BEP and (some) PACMAD lineages suggests it likely retained a function for at least some portion of this time. As the number of available grass genomes increases, a better estimate of the timing of gene duplication and loss events will be obtained. As gene duplication provides a foundation for subfunctionalization and neofunctionalization of gene function, comparative genomic approaches that exploit synteny relationships should increase our power to detect signatures of selection.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>The parallel evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis, a complex trait that requires the co-option and redeployment of enzymes from a wide range of biochemical pathways, as well as significant modifications of leaf anatomy, has long been a scientific puzzle. Here, we present the genome sequence of a C<sub>3</sub> species from within a clade rich in C<sub>4</sub> origins, the PACMAD grasses. We definitively show that despite having many C<sub>4</sub> relatives, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> uses the C<sub>3</sub> pathway for carbon fixation by coupling anatomical and physiological characteristics with sequence-based approaches. We demonstrate the usefulness of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> as a C<sub>3</sub> species for investigating C<sub>4</sub> evolution in the panicoid grasses through a variety of analyses. <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is also well placed for future studies of abiotic stress tolerance and adaption to temperate climates. The majority of panicoid grasses are native to tropical or subtropical environments. As a close relative indigenous to the continental United States, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> may be able to provide insight into the mechanisms employed by natural selection to develop cold and freezing tolerance in panicoid grass species, knowledge that would be of great value to cold sensitive panicoid crops such as <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, and <i>S. officinarum</i>. To the best of our knowledge, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> is only the third non-cultivated grass species with a sequenced genome, and the first C<sub>3</sub> grass in the otherwise C<sub>4</sub>-rich PACMAD clade.</p><p>Importantly, the results presented here highlight how comparisons among multiple closely related species produce smaller candidate gene lists that may be linked to a trait than pairwise comparisons between individual species. This is especially true when traits have evolved in parallel. Until recently, the time-consuming and expensive process of genome sequencing meant genome assemblies were only available for economically significant plant species (and a small number of widely used models). The uneven evolutionary distribution and small number of model/crop species limited the usefulness of comparative approaches. Today, researchers can select species based on informative phenotypes or phylogenetic positions and generate genomic data as needed. As scientists move beyond pairwise comparisons and begin to seek answers from large clusters of related species with sequenced genomes, new comparative genomics tools and statistical approaches will need to be developed.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant material</h3><p>A wild-grown plant was collected from the Shaw Nature Reserve, in Grays Summit, MO. A voucher specimen, <i>Kellogg 1175</i>, is deposited at the herbarium of the Missouri Botanical Garden (MO). Locality data are available at <a href="http://www.tropicos.org/Specimen/100315254">http://www.tropicos.org/Specimen/100315254</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Chloroplast gene sequences from <i>rbcL</i>, <i>ndhF</i>, and <i>matK</i> used for the phylogenetic analysis were aligned manually, and the best-fit model was estimated as GTR+G+I by RAxML using all sites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:13123." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3171">68</a>]. The Bayesian analysis was performed using MrBayes v3.2, with 5 million generations, sampling every 1000 generations. Substitution rates and state frequencies used a Dirichlet prior. The gamma distribution was approximated using four categories.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Histological analysis</h3><p>Two-millimeter strips were cut crosswise to the leaf of mature <i>D. oligosanthes</i> plants and were fixed for 2 h in 2 % gluteraldehyde in 100 mM, pH 6.8, PIPES buffer at room temperature. The leaves were then washed three times in 100 mM, pH 6.8, PIPES buffer. The leaves were post-fixed in buffered osmium tetroxide for 1.5 h and rinsed three times in water. The leaves were dehydrated in an ethanol/acetone series as follow: 5, 10, 20, 30, 50, 75, 95 % ETOH for 20 min each, followed by 30 min in 100 % ETOH, 15 min in 100 % acetone, and a second 45-min incubation in fresh 100 % acetone. The leaves were infiltrated with Spurs resin (Cat. no. RT14300, Electron Microscopy Science) dissolved in 100 % acetone as follows: 5 % 12 h, 10 % 12 h, 25 % 24 h, 50 % 24 h, 75 % 24 h, 100 % 24 h. The leaves were then embedded in 100 % resin and incubated at 60 C for two days. Resin blocks were cut into 1 M sections using a Leica Ultracut UCT microtome. Slides were stained in 1 % toluidine blue solution (Cat. No. T-140, Spectrum), and then sealed with a No. 1.5 cover glass and Permount (Cat. No. SP15-100, Fisher Scientific). Images were acquired using a Nikon E800 microscope with a 60X PCAN APO, 1.4NA oil immersion phase objective, Digital capture Q-imaging Ratiga 1300 camera, coupled with an LCD color filter, and Q-imaging software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Gas exchange measurements</h3><p>Net rates of CO<sub>2</sub> assimilation were measured on young, fully expanded leaves using the LI-COR 6400XT gas exchange system (LI-COR Biosciences). Measurements were made at 25 C and an irradiance of 1500 mol quanta m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>
                           <sub>.</sub> CO<sub>2</sub> response curves were measured at 25, 19.4, 16.6, 13.5, 7.6, 4.7, 36.7, 60.4, and 140.3 Pa. After gas exchange measurements were complete, leaf material was flash frozen and stored at 80  C for enzyme assays.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Enzyme assays</h3><p>Approximately 2 cm<sup>2</sup> leaf material was ground on ice using a mortar and pestle in 1 mL of 50 mM HEPES (pH 7.8), 1 % (v/v) polyvinylpolypyrrolidone, 1 mM EDTA, 10 mM dithiothreitol, 0.1 % (v/v) Triton X-100, and 2 % (v/v) protease inhibitor cocktail (Sigma-Aldrich). Crude extracts were centrifuged at 4  C for 1 min at 17,000 g, and supernatant was collected for the CA, Rubisco, and PEPC assays.</p><p>CA assays were performed at 25 C in 2 mL of assay buffer (CO<sub>2</sub>-free 100 mM EPPS-NaOH, pH 8.0, 10 mM DTT). CA activity was measured using a membrane inlet mass spectrometer to measure the rates of <sup>18</sup>O<sub>2</sub> exchange from labeled <sup>13</sup>C<sup>18</sup>O<sub>2</sub> to H<sub>2</sub>
                           <sup>16</sup>O with a total carbon concentration of 1 mM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Badger MR, Price GD. Carbonic anhydrase activity associated with the cyanobacterium Synechococcus PCC7942. Plant Physiol. 1989;89:5160." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3234">69</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Cousins AB, Badger MR, von Caemmerer S. C4 photosynthetic isotope exchange in NAD-ME- and NADP-ME-type grasses. J Exp Bot. 2008;59:1695703." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3237">71</a>]. The hydration rates were calculated from the enhancement in the rate of <sup>18</sup>O loss over the uncatalyzed rate by applying the non-enzymatic first-order rate constant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Jenkins CLD, Furbank RT, Hatch MD. Inorganic carbon diffusion between C4 mesophyll and bundle sheath cells - direct bundle sheath CO2 assimilation in intact leaves in the presence of an inhibitor of the C4 pathway. Plant Physiol. 1989;91:135663." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3243">72</a>].</p><p>Rubisco activity was spectrophotometrically measured in 1 mL of assay buffer (100 mM EPPS-NaOH (pH 8.0), 20 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 1 mM EDTA, 1 mM ATP, 5 mM creatine phosphate, 20 mM NaHCO<sub>3</sub>, 0.5 mM ribulose 1,5-bisphosphate, 0.2 mM NADH) containing coupling enzymes (12.5 units mL<sup>1</sup> creatine phosphokinase, 250 units mL<sup>1</sup> CA, 22.5 units mL<sup>1</sup> phosphoglycerokinase, 20 units mL<sup>1</sup> glyceraldehyde-3-phosphodehydrogenase, 56 units mL<sup>1</sup> triose isomerase, and 20 units mL<sup>1</sup> glycerol-3-phosphodehydrogenase). Rubisco activity was calculated from the consumption of NADH, which was monitored via the change in absorption at 340 nm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Walker B, Ariza LS, Kaines S, Badger MR, Cousins AB. Temperature response of in vivo Rubisco kinetics and mesophyll conductance in Arabidopsis thaliana: comparisons to Nicotiana tabacum. Plant Cell Environ. 2013;36:210819." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3266">73</a>].</p><p>PEPC activity was assayed in 1 mL of assay buffer (100 mM EPPS-NaOH (pH 8.0), 20 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 1 mM EDTA, 5 mM NaHCO<sub>3</sub>, 0.2 mM NADH, 5 mM D-glucose-6-phosphate, 12 units mL<sup>1</sup> malate dehydrogenase, and 4 mM phosphoenolpyruvate). NADH consumption was monitored at 340 nm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Sun W, Ubierna N, Ma JY, Cousins AB. The influence of light quality on C4 photosynthesis under steady-state conditions in Zea mays and Miscanthusxgiganteus: changes in rates of photosynthesis but not the efficiency of the CO2 concentrating mechanism. Plant Cell Environ. 2012;35:98293." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3278">74</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Modeling</h3><p>The CO<sub>2</sub> response curves for C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis were modeled according to von Caemmerer (2000). The C<sub>3</sub> model was matched to gas exchange data from <i>D. oligosanthes</i> using a <i>V</i>
                           <sub>
                    <i>cmax</i>
                  </sub>=77 mol m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup> and a <i>J</i>
                           <sub>
                    <i>max</i>
                  </sub>=144 mol m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>. C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis was modeled using <i>V</i>
                           <sub>
                    <i>cmax</i>
                  </sub>=35 mol m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup> to have comparable maximum photosynthetic rates to those measured in <i>D. oligosanthes</i>. All other modeling parameters were taken from von Caemmerer ([<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="von Caemmerer S. Biochemical models of leaf photosynthesis. Collingwood: CSIRO Publishing; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3347">34</a>]; see Tables 2.3 and 4.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Stable isotopes</h3><p>Dried leaf material was placed in tin capsules and combusted in a hydrogen/carbon/nitrogen elemental analyzer (ECS 4010; Costech Analytical) to determine carbon isotopic composition.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Growth conditions for physiological measurements</h3><p>Plants were grown in a controlled-environment growth chamber (Biochambers; GC-16, Winnipeg, Manitoba, Canada) with a 14-h photoperiod and a photosynthetic photon flux density of 500 mol m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup> at leaf height. Relative humidity was maintained at approximately 40 %. Day/night air temperatures were 28 and 18 C, respectively. Plants were watered as needed and fertilized weekly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>All DNA used for sequencing was taken from a single F<sub>2</sub> plant descended from the original collection, <i>Kellogg 1175</i>. Following the recommended Allpaths-LG sequencing protocol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:15138." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3383">43</a>], a 180 bp insert DNA-seq library and 5 kb mate pair DNA libraries were prepared and sequenced at the Cornell University Sequencing Core. Each library was sequenced twice in 2100 bp paired end sequencing lanes. Both libraries were quality trimmed using Trimmomatic [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Bolger AM, Lohse M, Usadel B. Trimmomatic: A flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:211420." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3386">75</a>]. The mate pair libraries were subject to a second quality control step in which polymerase chain reaction duplicate reads (defined as cases where the first 20 base pairs for both forward and reverse reads were identical between independently sequenced clusters) were removed using a simple python script. The resulting dataset was assembled using Allpaths-LG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:15138." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3389">43</a>], with the ploidy file set to 2 (diploid), and otherwise default settings. Final scaffolding was conducted with SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:5789." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3393">44</a>] utilizing long mate pair libraries sequenced on an Illumina MiSeq using 2150 sequencing.</p><p>A subset of the total sequencing data (2.3 Gigabytes) was used to assemble the chloroplast genome. SPAdes v.3.1.0 was used to make the initial assembly using the only-assembler option with k-mer sizes of 55 and 87. The SPAdes contigs were blasted (blastn) against the <i>Z. mays</i> chloroplast genome (NC_001666.2) and mitochondrial genome (NC_007982.1) with an e-value cutoff of 1e-40. The contigs were identified as plastid or mitochondrial using a custom script to determine the optimal e-value and longest single hit length. Plastid-like sequences were then put into Sequencher (Genecodes 5.2.4). Ends of contigs were identified and were trimmed by 100 base pairs to remove potentially misassembled regions. Twenty base pair sequences from the end of trimmed contigs were used to search all raw reads for exact matching sequence using grep. This was repeated with the reverse complement of the sequence. The matching reads were aligned to the contigs in Sequencher and used to extend the contig ends. This process continued until all gaps were closed. The sequence was orientated for the typical representation of large single copy, inverted repeat B, small single copy, and inverted repeat A. Jellyfish v.2.1.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Marcais G, Kingsford C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:76470." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3402">51</a>] was used to estimate 20-mer abundance from the reads and these abundance values were matched to the assembled plastome to determine a sliding window coverage across the assembly. No anomalies in coverage were identified and the assembly was considered complete. Annotation of the plastome was completed using DOGMA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wyman SK, Jansen RK, Boore JL. Automatic annotation of organellar genomes with DOGMA. Bioinformatics. 2004;20:32525." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3405">52</a>]. A graphical representation of the annotated plastome was created using Circos v.0.66 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19:163945." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3408">53</a>] as implemented in Verdant (verdant.iplantcollaborative.org).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Gene annotation</h3><p>Gene annotation was conducted using Maker2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3419">46</a>]. For each of <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>, and <i>S. italica</i>, the primaryTranscriptOnly files for cDNA and protein sequences were downloaded from phytozome and alignments of these files against the <i>D. oligosanthes</i> genome assembly were used for the evidence based portion of Maker2s analysis (Maker2 also incorporates ab initio gene prediction approaches into its final analysis).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Leaf gradient construction</h3><p>Plants were sown in MetroMix360 and grown in Conviron BDW-40 controlled chambers under 500 mol/m<sup>2</sup>/s of light with 12-h light/dark cycles at 31 C and 22 C, respectively, and a constant 50 % relative humidity. Leaf tissue was sampled after the second leaf ligule formed but before the third leaf had fully expanded. This corresponds to three weeks after planting for <i>D. oligosanthes</i>, and nine days after planting for <i>S. viridis</i>. Samples were collected in the morning 2 h after the lights turned on and were immediately flash frozen in liquid nitrogen. Four plants were pooled for each segment replicate of <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and ten plants were pooled for each segment replicate of <i>S. viridis</i>. TriPure Isolation Reagent (Sigma) was used to extract total RNA following the manufacturers recommendations. Libraries were prepared according to Wang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3465">55</a>]. <i>D. oligosanthes</i> libraries were sequenced on a HiSeq2000 using a 100 bp paired end run and <i>S. viridis</i> libraries were sequenced on a HiSeq2500 using a 100 bp single end run.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Gene expression analysis</h3><p>Raw Illumina reads from RNA-seq libraries were subjected to quality trimming using CutAdapt [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Martin M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnetjournal. 2011;17:102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3482">76</a>] with a minimum quality score of 20 and a minimum read length of 25 bp after trimming. Trimmed reads were aligned to reference genomes (<i>S. bicolor</i> v1.4, <i>D. oligosanthes</i> v1.0, and <i>S. italica</i> v2.1) using [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Wu TD, Nacu S. Fast and SNP-tolerant detection of complex variants and splicing in short reads. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:87381." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3494">77</a>], allowing splicing over canonical RNA-splice sites, a maximum number of reported alignments per read of 10, a maximum mismatch rate of 3 (allowing up to three SNPs or one SNP and one InDel). FPKM values were calculated using Cufflinks [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Trapnell C, Williams BA, Pertea G, Mortazavi A, Kwan G, van Baren MJ, et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat Biotechnol. 2010;28:5115." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3498">78</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Leaf gradient and TF analysis</h3><p>A minimum FPKM threshold was applied to the Cufflinks output. This threshold stipulates that a gene must have an FPKM value equal to or greater than one in at least one segment of any replicate. Correlation matrices and heat map visualizations were constructed using R software packages (code available upon request). CoGe Blast and CoGe SynFind tools were used to find core C<sub>4</sub> gene and TF orthologs for the different grass species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lyons E, Freeling M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 2008;53:66173." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3511">47</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Lyons E, Pedersen B, Kane J, Alam M, Ming R, Tang H, et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 2008;148:177281." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3514">48</a>]. Segments 1, 4, 9, and 14 from <i>Z. mays</i> and 1, 3, 6, and 9 from <i>O. sativa</i> from previously published datasets [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Wang L, Czedik-Eysenberg A, Mertz RA, Si Y, Tohge T, Nunes-Nesi A, et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2014;32:115865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3524">55</a>] were compared to the four segment gradient data for <i>D. oligosanthes</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> generated in this study. Transcription factor leaf gradient expression profiles from the different species were overlaid and compared by eye.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">PEPC amino acid tree</h3><p>The coding sequence for Do021545.1 contained an alternative splice site and had to be manually curated with raw sequencing reads. Amino acid sequences are listed in Supplementary Material. Amino acid sequences were aligned using the Muscle aligner. The evolutionary history was inferred by using the Maximum Likelihood method based on the JTT matrix-based model. The bootstrap consensus tree inferred from 500 replicates was taken to represent the evolutionary history of the taxa analyzed. Branches corresponding to partitions reproduced in less than 50 % bootstrap replicates are collapsed. The percentage of replicate trees in which the associated taxa clustered together in the bootstrap test (500 replicates) are shown next to the branches. The analysis involved 12 amino acid sequences. All positions containing gaps and missing data were eliminated. There was a total of 863 positions in the final dataset. Evolutionary analyses were conducted in MEGA (version 6.06) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Tamura K, Stecher G, Peterson D, Filipski A, Kumar S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:27259." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3541">79</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">
                           <i>ca</i> gene family</h3><p>RACE was performed using the GeneRacer kit (Invitrogen, cat#L1502-01) using RNA extracted from leaf tissue. Ten independent clones were sequenced per RACE reaction. <i>ca</i> gene coding sequences were aligned using the Muscle codon aligner in MEGA (version 6.06) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Tamura K, Stecher G, Peterson D, Filipski A, Kumar S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:27259." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3559">79</a>]. <i>ca1</i> sequences from <i>Z. mays</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i> have duplicated active site domains. These gene fusions were treated as two separate genes in the analysis. Alignment gaps were removed and then the alignment was loaded into Cipres Science Gateway (<a href="http://www.phylo.org/">http://www.phylo.org/</a>), where RaxML [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:13123." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-1080-3#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d33287e3579">68</a>] was used to generate a maximum-likelihood tree.</p></div></div></section>

Shyu for generously providing the <i>S. viridis</i> leaf image.</p>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Funding</h3>
                <p>This work was supported by a Department of Energy - Division of Biosciences Fellowship of the Life Sciences Research Foundation to AJS (DE-FG02-12ER16337), a NSF PGRP fellowship to JCS (IOS-1306738), a grant from the National Science Foundation (MCB-1127017) and funding from the Donald Danforth Plant Science Center to TPB, a grant from the Division of Chemical Sciences, Geosciences and Biosciences, Office of Basic Energy Sciences, Photosynthetic Systems (DE-FG02-09ER16062) and with instrumentation from an NSF Major Research Instrumentation grant to ABC (#0923562).</p>
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar2">Availability of data and materials</h3>
                <p>The Dichanthelium v.1 genome assembly and gene model annotations are freely available from NCBI under accession number LWDX00000000. The raw sequencing reads used for the genome assembly have been submitted to NCBIs SRA under accession SRP067405, SRS1207314, SRX1483064, SRR3018854, SRX1488867, SRR3018860. RNA-seq data from the <i>D. oligosanthes,</i> and <i>S. viridis</i> leaf gradients have been deposited in the NCBI SRA (SRP058858 and SRP063517, respectively).</p>
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar3">Authors contributions</h3>
                <p>AS designed experiments and analyzed data, JS designed experiments and analyzed data, SW performed the anatomical analysis and collected leaf gradient data, AK performed physiology experiments and analyzed data, MM assembled and annotated the chloroplast genome, YS conceived the sequencing strategy and collected data, AC designed physiology experiments and analyzed data, EK provided the accession, designed experiments, and did phylogenetic analyses, TB designed experiments and analyzed data. All authors contributed, read, and approved the final manuscript.</p>
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar4">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The Donald Danforth Plant Science Center is pursuing a patent on behalf of AS and TB on aspects related to the findings in this manuscript.</p>
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar5">Ethics approval and consent to participate</h3>
                <p>Not applicable.</p>
